    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5899377189097196090"><i>Justine</i> was the Marquis de Sade's first novella, written in 1787, whilst imprisoned for two weeks in the Bastille. Although published anonymously, de Sade was eventually indicted for blasphemy and obscenity (without trial) for the authorship of <i>Justine</i> at the behest of Napoleon Bonaparte.<br/><br/>Who suffers in the pursuit of desire?<br/><br/>The Countess de Lorsange reveals her history, in </span>
  <span id="freeText5899377189097196090" style="display:none"><i>Justine</i> was the Marquis de Sade's first novella, written in 1787, whilst imprisoned for two weeks in the Bastille. Although published anonymously, de Sade was eventually indicted for blasphemy and obscenity (without trial) for the authorship of <i>Justine</i> at the behest of Napoleon Bonaparte.<br /><br />Who suffers in the pursuit of desire?<br /><br />The Countess de Lorsange reveals her history, in a tavern, to a young woman named Therese; where a young girl and her sister fight a battle of morality. Set in a period before the French Revolution, Justine shows the battle of virtue versus vice, where earning your keep takes on fresh connotations, and a titled lady holds a lifetime of illicit secrets.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5899377189097196090" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_6762429" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6762429" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6762429?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6762429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14632995669304431897">This Justine she had like the all time worst luck, even worse than this girl Tiffany that I know who broke her leg on the first day of a three week ski holiday, it sounds so sad unless you know her in which case it practically proves there's a God right there. But anyway. I was reading this book at breakfast and that was a good laugh in itself cause people thought it must be a highly educational book because it was written before the Ark and I'm not saying they were <i>wrong</i> neither because some of</span>
  <span id="freeText14632995669304431897" style="display:none">This Justine she had like the all time worst luck, even worse than this girl Tiffany that I know who broke her leg on the first day of a three week ski holiday, it sounds so sad unless you know her in which case it practically proves there's a God right there. But anyway. I was reading this book at breakfast and that was a good laugh in itself cause people thought it must be a highly educational book because it was written before the Ark and I'm not saying they were <i>wrong</i> neither because some of it <i>was very educational</i> if you know whatb I mean but I got to one bit and I blurted cocopops all down my nose it was hilarious and so so gross. So you know, don't read Justine at breakfast. So this must be like <i>French Psycho</i> you know like American Psycho only its really old ass so it's <i>French Old Ass Psycho</i> lol. You know we have a lot of fast food shops in towns these days like KFC and those, well in the before the Ark France they must have had instead of fast french food a whole lot of whip shops ROTFLMWO (yeah roll on the floor laughing my whip off). It was just sick. So I guess we don't need books like this any more because we have modern videos like <i>Tumbling Doll of Flesh</i> or <i>The Human centipede</i> which rocks, you must see that, and also many computer games like Remains of the Bitches, and so you don't have to drag your eyes through all these words, it's the same. <br /><br />Oh yeah I got to tell you one person who saw me reading this and I wont say who asked me why I was reading about the singer and I said who and they said Sade, you know, she's pretty famous. So I just said oh I'm a big fan, got all her stuff. Those are the kind of people I have to live with, just so you know. But yeah, Justine, man I was just howling. Stuff coming down my nose and everything.</span>

<div id="review_84763507" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84763507" />
    <a title="Traveller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3145526-traveller"><img alt="Traveller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423246742p2/3145526.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84763507?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Traveller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Traveller" href="/user/show/3145526-traveller">Traveller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=banned-books">banned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=review-pending">review-pending</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84763507" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9356571340635270996">*4,5 out of 10 spiky stars<br/><br/>I suspect that I expected to find something similar to smutty contemporary sado-masochistic literature, and was therefore quite pleasantly surprised stylistically speaking. Having watched documentaries about De Sade which depicted him as a dribbling sot in an institution for the insane, I had expected... well... something much less intelligent than this. De Sade is obviously no fool, and his arguments are intelligently put.<br/><br/>In this novel, De Sade still conforms to Victor</span>
  <span id="freeText9356571340635270996" style="display:none">*4,5 out of 10 spiky stars<br /><br />I suspect that I expected to find something similar to smutty contemporary sado-masochistic literature, and was therefore quite pleasantly surprised stylistically speaking. Having watched documentaries about De Sade which depicted him as a dribbling sot in an institution for the insane, I had expected... well... something much less intelligent than this. De Sade is obviously no fool, and his arguments are intelligently put.<br /><br />In this novel, De Sade still conforms to Victorian norms in the respect that even libertine, rebellious De Sade felt he had to wrap everything he said in euphemistic terms, in spite of the fact that the content of the novel and the gist of his rhetoric would indeed have been wildly shocking to most Victorian sensibilities. <br /><br />Compared to certain contemporary literature, such as some of the works of authors like Palanuik, for example in his novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1840511.Snuff" title="Snuff by Chuck Palahniuk" rel="nofollow noopener">Snuff</a>, also, various writings of J.G. Ballard, Samuel R. Delany's novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85865.Hogg" title="Hogg by Samuel R. Delany" rel="nofollow noopener">Hogg</a> and others like the work of Brett Easton Ellis, Justine is not so very shocking in the contemporary landscape despite De Sade's reputation.<br /><br />Of course, De Sade's later novels, especially <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6351885.The_120_Days_of_Sodom" title="The 120 Days of Sodom by Marquis de Sade" rel="nofollow noopener">The 120 Days of Sodom</a>, truly do contain disgusting material, even by contemporary standards.<br /><br />If one feels you really MUST read De Sade, I guess this is one of the most accessible ones, but, even though it is De Sade 'lite' it does nonetheless contain quite a bit of sadism, and poor little Justine does seem a bit of a sucker for punishment, the quintessential 'victim'.<br /><br />Justine is the quintessential sad sac of the ages. De Sade paints her as a poor meek little thing who, according to him, deserves her end because of her continued piousness. His rhetoric is of course completely distorted, but one does get a sense that she (possibly unwittingly or subconsciously) invites victimhood. I hope people won't find it too cruel if I say that her end was actually quite a humorous piece of Deux Ex Machina. De Sade subverts a very common trope, by having a punishment that is usually visited upon sinners, strike down the innocent instead.<br /><br />One often has to wonder why a lightning bolt never came down from heaven to strike the sinful Marquis himself down.<br /><br />I liked that at least De Sade does not try to justify sadism as anything else than what it really is. He openly rebels against 'piety' with his proposed hedonistic rhetoric. The sadists and hedonists in the novel are what they are, fully and completely, and they don't make excuses for it, or pretend to 'love' their victims. They don't love their victims. They are most definitely not 'relationship' material, not poor misunderstood whelps who can be recycled if you just loved them enough. ...and perhaps this is precicely why de Sade is so reviled. He removes all the cobwebby romanticism, the smarmy treacle that these proclivities tend to be marinated in in popular fiction.<br /><br />I don't agree with most of De Sade's philosophy, but from a literary stylistic point of view the book is probably a sight better than 50 Shades of Grey.<br /><br />Proper review to follow when time permits.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9356571340635270996" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_785901816" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/785901816" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7228542-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462332402p2/7228542.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/785901816?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/7228542-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7228542-mary?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7228542-mary?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7228542-mary?shelf=don-t-like">don-t-like</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7228542-mary?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer785901816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer184187556399267328">First impressions:<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1387250689ra/7560745.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Marquis de Sade...you are one crazy m*****f*****. <br/><br/>So I had learned about Marquis de Sade, or at least first heard of him after watching the movie Quills and I immediately became really interested. Yes, his works are incredibly graphic and really go off the deep end, but he was added to my bucket list of authors I wanted to read just for the sake of reading.<br/><br/>After doing a little research I chose Justine, a classic, and one of his more famous stories to give a try at but it was a </span>
  <span id="freeText184187556399267328" style="display:none">First impressions:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1387250689ra/7560745.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Marquis de Sade...you are one crazy m*****f*****. <br><br>So I had learned about Marquis de Sade, or at least first heard of him after watching the movie Quills and I immediately became really interested. Yes, his works are incredibly graphic and really go off the deep end, but he was added to my bucket list of authors I wanted to read just for the sake of reading.<br><br>After doing a little research I chose Justine, a classic, and one of his more famous stories to give a try at but it was a failure. I got either really bored or completely confused. It took me up until a couple of weeks ago to finally get his prose which is a bunch of active narration and then about 10 pages of speeches/sermons articulating his ideals. Ah ha! So away I went onto this journey, knowing that this was going to be one heck of a messed up story but curiosity is a powerful thing....<br><br>To start things off, before I really get into my opinion on Justine, a quick summary and yes this will contain spoilers, but I include them so everyone can understand where the heck I'm coming from here.<br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>Justine takes place in 17th or 18th century France and revolves around two sisters, Juliet and Justine. Both orphaned just a few years before they could be married off split their inheritance and separate ways due to their highly contrasting ideals. Juliet believes that to get what you want, you must be willing to do anything. Justine on the other hand believes that as long as you maintain perfect virtue, God will reward you with a perfect life. The sisters separate and Juliet immediately gives her inheritance to a Madame of a brothel where over the years she becomes famous for her skills and discretion. She marries several times and her fortune multiplies as each husband 'mysteriously' dies soon after the wedding and she gains their inheritances. However she soon meets her match in her mid thirties and both decide to have a fun trip around the country side when they pull up to an inn and see the most beautiful young woman bound in handcuffs and off to Paris to be hanged. Curiosity ignites in Juliet and she pays the jailers to stay the night at the inn to rest so that they can hear the girl's story.<br><br>Shyly the girl introduces herself as Therese and tells them straightaway she will not reveal her lineage or the circumstances of her childhood but would rather begin when she became orphaned and her fate with vice began when she was at the age of 15. Her first employer she explains, hired her as a maid and took her in due to pity and on recommendation of a family friend. Soon though, Therese learns that her employer is robbing people and is asked to join. Refusing out of her love of virtue she is fired and blamed for the robberies that had occurred at the residence. On the run she is taken in by a group of bandits whom ask her to join them or die. Again she explains she cannot due to her virtue and they say she must stay with them since she knows who they are. An orgy then takes place but gets to keep her virginity after pleading mercy to the thieves. Yay?<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1387253241ra/7561074.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The next day, the thieves return to their lair bringing a young man with them whom they had just robbed. Nursing the man, Therese and him run away in the night where he promises to hire her as a maid after hearing of her misfortunes. But the next day he hits her over the head, rapes her and flees with all of her money and clothes. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1387253241ra/7561075.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Walking aimlessly in the woods she encounters two male lovers, the richer of the two hiring her as a maid in trade for her silence on his debaucheries. For two years she works for this Lord Asshole (no pun intended) where he confides he will poison his aunt to get his inheritance sooner. Following her sense of virtue she confesses the plan to the aunt, but it is too late. She is soon after poisoned and the blame once more put onto Therese where she must run away into the woods. The Lord however soon catches up to her and makes his dogs torture her for an hour until she faints from the bites. Leaving her for dead, Therese awakens and travels to the nearest town and seeks a doctor. The doctor however is also crazy messed up for after healing her, she finds out he is really into BDSM with kids, including his daughter. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1387253241ra/7561076.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Again she tries to rescue the victim of vice, the daughter, and again fails. Therese again runs away and on her travels seeks a monastery to confess everything that had happened to her. A women in the woods directs the way to the closest one and it isn't until nightfall Therese finds the place. An older monk takes her confession and after questioning whether anyone knew she was there or if she had any relatives, kidnaps her and takes her into a hidden prison near the monastery where four monks keep 32 women as prisoners for their every whim. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1387253241ra/7561077.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Therese is raped and tortured by all four monks for a year before breaking out and immediately getting kidnapped AGAIN. This time by two young men who take her to a Count's chateau to be a maid for his ailing wife. Therese agrees, just happy to be away from the monastery, before she learns that the reason no maid stays around long is because the Count loves to bleed people. Yes, bleed people. Every four days he bleeds his wife close to death for his enjoyment. Hence why he goes through so many over the years.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1387253241ra/7561078.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Again Therese barely escapes but is then kidnapped YET AGAIN but this time by coin counter-fitters who torture and rape her for a year. Eventually though they get caught, Therese gets arrested but let go since the judge believes her pitiful story. Some more harrowing things happen including a proposal and then instant poisoning before Therese ends up trying to save a baby from a fire, trips and the baby falls into it. She then gets charged for the murder of the baby and ends up arrested, yet again, and on her way to Paris for the execution. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1387253911ra/7561251.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Throughout all of the Juliet begins to guess who Therese really is and both figure out that she is Justine! Her sister! Much rejoicing commences and Juliet vows to save Justine from all of her troubles and over the months nurses her back to health. However Therese soon gets depressed knowing that some calamity will soon befall her and sure enough she gets struck by lightening. The end.<br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>Okay! Now that we have gotten through all the nitty-and-gritty here is my opinion. What a load of garbage. Seriously why is this still in print anymore? Its like the Kardashians, famous for being famous and just as pathetic and sad. Basically the Marquis tried his hardest to not only gross out his audience but piss them off. The whole story's premise is basically that there cannot be good in the world without evil, therefore it is better to be evil to provide for the good. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1492149865i/22486919.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Whatever. Did not like, really just finished the darn thing because I never really want to give up on a book. So many times Justine should have died but didn't and so many times she was just soo brainless. I mean if you keep getting kidnapped on your nightly walks, maybe...just maybe you SHOULDN'T take nightly walks!! Daytime girl. DAYTIME. Ugh. Waste of time but hey I crossed it off on my bucket list. <br><br>Don't really recommend for anyone unless you are just really curious what the heck it is after seeing the movie Quills. It's not often that I give such a bad review for a book because I try to look at both the pros and cons, but being a sh*tty story for the sake of being sh*tty story does not make it a good story. It makes it sh*tty. There I said it. <br><br>Boom! I'm out.<br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_486096043" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/486096043" />
    <a title="Claudia Hernandez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15800243-claudia-hernandez"><img alt="Claudia Hernandez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464995940p2/15800243.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/486096043?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claudia Hernandez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claudia Hernandez" href="/user/show/15800243-claudia-hernandez">Claudia Hernandez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer486096043" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3251026736097137693">It is precisely the Misfortunes of Virtue which Sade illuminates in Justine. Virtue, personified in Justine, is perpetually confronted by misfortune, and subdued by an omnipresent force, which, as expected from the Marquis, drips animalistic eroticism. Justine is, inarguably, a novel on depravity, but beyond its blatant decadent literature, Justine is also to be seen and appreciated as a historical document -- a passionate chronicle and assessment of a culture not governed by law, but through th</span>
  <span id="freeText3251026736097137693" style="display:none">It is precisely the Misfortunes of Virtue which Sade illuminates in Justine. Virtue, personified in Justine, is perpetually confronted by misfortune, and subdued by an omnipresent force, which, as expected from the Marquis, drips animalistic eroticism. Justine is, inarguably, a novel on depravity, but beyond its blatant decadent literature, Justine is also to be seen and appreciated as a historical document -- a passionate chronicle and assessment of a culture not governed by law, but through the despotic monarchy of King Louis XVI. It is, essentially, a work of political philosophy and a critique of Revolutionary France.<br /><br />Despite its ardent imagery the novel expands beyond the prurient joyride evident in its erotic style. That is to say, Justine is a candid rebuttal of Rousseau’s views of the noble savage and the social contract. The Marquis expounds man's unfettered indulgence as being fueled by momentary impulse and pleasure-- blind to consequence, or affliction to others. In the Sadean demimonde, characters (and people), were placed in either a position to impose their desires or as the objects upon which the desires of others were imposed upon -- occasionally a character enjoys the magnificence of the two. The Marquis’s savage is not noble in any perceivable sense, but rather merely a bundle of lustful appetites sulking underneath the patina of civilization, anticipating the occasion to be satiated. <br /><br />Sade not only boldly decorated the macabre side of humanity, but he also glorified it and gave it a name—libertinism. He did so by drafting the most luxurious, nauseating, and magnetizing piece of writing since Ovid’s Erotic Poems. Justine is, arguably, the most extraordinary novel of the Marquis; the horrors narrated evoke a plethora of emotions in the reader—one minute inducing uninhibited sexual arousal, shortly after the arousal gives way to anguish, anguish to desperation, desperation into repudiation, until repudiation finally precipitates into cholera. It’s grotesque, inhumane, fascinating, and extraordinarily compelling. <br /><br />Admittedly, I was simultaneously horrified and strangely captivated by Justine. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3251026736097137693" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7587740612110853098" style="display:none">Popsugar Challenge 2020 - A book featuring one of the seven deadly sins <br /><br />Oh Justine! Up until i read this I would have said Tess of the D'Urberville's was the unluckiest woman in classic literature but I take it all back. JUSTINE!!!<br /><br />I have a fascination with Marquis De Sade, one of the first documented Libertines and one of the earliest writers of BDSM and same sex relations. 17th century French literature is extraordinary.  <br /><br />Triggers warnings all over this, this is not a typical classic novel where disturbing scenes are alluded too, this contains on the page graphic gang bangs and rape.  Modern day dark genre fiction pales beside De Sade which should not be a surprise since the word sadism was derived from his name. <br /><br />For those interested in trying this out the first volume is on Spotify narrated by Susan Penhaligon which just her voice alone is extremely enjoyable. <br /><br />Justine I shall see you again, this is one book I would reread.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer3004514669311944975">As Will Self puts it succinctly <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=gZLtUWJhM0s"' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>, paraphrased: “I believe that humans are innately evil. It’s called egoism. When egoism hypertrophies, when it grows and grows like a cancer, then that person becomes a full-blown evil person.” Enter the uncompromising realm of the Marquis and the sequence of cartoon abuses heaped upon the virginal (anti?)heroine for over 250pp, translated (if Paul Bryant is to be believed), by the former (disgraced) songwriter of the Mamas and the Papas, who also wrote a <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/152115.How_to_Read_Sade' title='How to Read Sade by John Phillips' rel='nofollow noopener'>mon</a></span>
  <span id="freeText3004514669311944975" style="display:none">As Will Self puts it succinctly <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=gZLtUWJhM0s&quot;" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>, paraphrased: “I believe that humans are innately evil. It’s called egoism. When egoism hypertrophies, when it grows and grows like a cancer, then that person becomes a full-blown evil person.” Enter the uncompromising realm of the Marquis and the sequence of cartoon abuses heaped upon the virginal (anti?)heroine for over 250pp, translated (if Paul Bryant is to be believed), by the former (disgraced) songwriter of the Mamas and the Papas, who also wrote a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/152115.How_to_Read_Sade" title="How to Read Sade by John Phillips" rel="nofollow noopener">monograph</a> on the Marquis (note: this is a different John Phillips), and the ludicrous belief that humans should cultivate their innate evil and flout laws and conventions, because God does not love you, and the light at the end of the tunnel is the light of an oncoming train, and so on, yaketynoway. Phillips’s introduction for this book’s status as a blistering satire on the hypocrisy of institutions, and the absolute corruption of absolute power is a convincing one, and since the tortures mount in their ludicrousness, one can read this as a parodic period piece and save one’s blushes. </span>

<div id="review_331551727" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/331551727" />
    <a title="Peter Brooks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5248302-peter-brooks"><img alt="Peter Brooks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506918675p2/5248302.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/331551727?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter Brooks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter Brooks" href="/user/show/5248302-peter-brooks">Peter Brooks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer331551727" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9614741011908551671">de Sade's unfortunate reputation goes before him. Certainly none of his books are ones that you'd recommend to a maiden aunt. Part of their reputation is well earned.<br/><br/>However, it is, I think, a pity to dismiss him simply as a pornographer. His books are an unusual read, but are stimulating. His fierce anti-clericism is refreshing, if, as with most of what he says, rather on the extreme level.<br/><br/>What I find most informative, though, are his discussions of morality, ethics and government. de Sade was </span>
  <span id="freeText9614741011908551671" style="display:none">de Sade's unfortunate reputation goes before him. Certainly none of his books are ones that you'd recommend to a maiden aunt. Part of their reputation is well earned.<br /><br />However, it is, I think, a pity to dismiss him simply as a pornographer. His books are an unusual read, but are stimulating. His fierce anti-clericism is refreshing, if, as with most of what he says, rather on the extreme level.<br /><br />What I find most informative, though, are his discussions of morality, ethics and government. de Sade was quite an enlightened proto-anarchist and, surprisingly, was, in many ways Epicurean. It is unfortunate that his fascinating political and social observations are mixed up with the salacious stuff, so it's difficult, for many people, to come to grips with them.<br /><br />If you're not squeamish, or easily offended, and are interested in a remarkable man, with an unusual perspective, I think de Sade is well worth reading!</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_714524073" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/714524073" />
    <a title="Spinster" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12181320-spinster"><img alt="Spinster" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346046895p2/12181320.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/714524073?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Spinster" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Spinster" href="/user/show/12181320-spinster">Spinster</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007300440" href="/book/show/6337204-justine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12181320-spinster?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer714524073" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11775102498199971883">I'm not sure what I was expecting from this book. Knowing de Sade's reputation I assumed Justine would be controversial and an interesting insight to the late 18th century life. Yes, I can see how and why it was controversial and yes, it was a sort of an insight to a late 18th century deviant's mind, but interesting? Not so much.<br/><br/>I think I expected more substance and depth to the story from Justine as a reading experience. It took me months to read it, mostly because I couldn't understand any of </span>
  <span id="freeText11775102498199971883" style="display:none">I'm not sure what I was expecting from this book. Knowing de Sade's reputation I assumed Justine would be controversial and an interesting insight to the late 18th century life. Yes, I can see how and why it was controversial and yes, it was a sort of an insight to a late 18th century deviant's mind, but interesting? Not so much.<br /><br />I think I expected more substance and depth to the story from Justine as a reading experience. It took me months to read it, mostly because I couldn't understand any of the characters, I strongly disagreed with many of the "philosophy" rants in the book and I suppose I was already too jaded for the shock value to have any effect. I do see why de Sade was and has become such a debated author and philosopher, but I doubt he'd get a following if he lived and wrote in today's world. You have to switch your brain a couple hundred years back before you can really see a point to this book.<br /><br />Justine is basically about good and evil, virtue and sin, good guys vs. bad guys. It's all very well, but it gets tiring after a while. The same scene gets repeated so many times that the reader (me at least) gets frustrated with all that obviousness. The heroine (possibly the dumbest chick ever to appear in a book) gets assaulted, imprisoned, raped and abused so many times and in so many ways that in the end you just don't care anymore. Justine is de Sade's personification of the perfect believer - she's virtuous and blindly faithful to her God, naive and easily manipulated, always willing to help others and never learning from her mistakes or past experiences.<br /><br />The so-called bad guys, on the other hand, are twisted and sadistic atheists, to them it's all about having their own pleasure with any means necessary and not giving a crap about anything or anyone else.<br /><br />And therein lies my biggest problem with the book. It seems that in de Sade's mind atheism is not so much about denying the possibility of God, but hating him. The abusers do monologues that last for pages, advising Justine to practically stomp on God and embrace the hedonistic lifestyle they all seem to be rooting for. She's like a creep magnet, finding every perv in France! Anyhow, I'm not impressed by the way de Sade insinuates that if you're not a virtuous believer, you have no morale. I think he failed to see the concepts of religion and morale in a bigger context and made a few rash conclusions based on his own experience.<br /><br />All in all, I think Justine was pretty much a collection of de Sade's own fantasies disguised with a few bits of philosophical debate.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11775102498199971883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_102262999" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/102262999" />
    <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova"><img alt="Jeannette Nikolova" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480677087p2/2574284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/102262999?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeannette Nikolova" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova">Jeannette Nikolova</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070354467" href="/book/show/960532.Justine_ou_les_malheurs_de_la_vertu">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer102262999&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer102262999" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6219539180284946240">Oh my God, this is one of the worst books I've read in my life. The writing is vulgar and repetitive and the author has a lexicon of 200 words and expressions, which he never strays away from. The male characters aren't really characters plural as much as one character repeated ten times. It's the very same man again and again - a sadist(har-har) who likes to have orgies with both females and males alike, and to give himself pleasure while torturing other people. Also, all the men in the book gi</span>
  <span id="freeText6219539180284946240" style="display:none">Oh my God, this is one of the worst books I've read in my life. The writing is vulgar and repetitive and the author has a lexicon of 200 words and expressions, which he never strays away from. The male characters aren't really characters plural as much as one character repeated ten times. It's the very same man again and again - a sadist(har-har) who likes to have orgies with both females and males alike, and to give himself pleasure while torturing other people. Also, all the men in the book give Justine the EXACT same speech about how it's so much easier to make a woman feel pain rather than pleasure. Even their descriptions are repetitive - they all seem cruel and disgusting, even though Justine ALWAYS realizes that after she's realized they really are cruel and disgusting. All the girls in the book are so "marvelous", "beautiful", "young", "stunning", "lovely", et cetera, et cetera. And not to mention Justine who's not really good, OR a victim. She may be forced to go somewhere, but all the things she does, she does them willingly. <br /><br />The book follows a VERY tiring pattern: Justine goes to someone for help. They offer her that help and in return they want sex. All of a sudden she's all innocent and would never do what they ask. They force her to. They tell her what they want, and she does it, willingly. So, it's not really even a matter of "forced". Who's description of good and innocent is that? And once she's about to be killed, a miracle happens and she's free. She goes to someone for help. They offer her that help and in return they want sex. AGAIN AND AGAIN. <br /><br />I don't know what people find so highly intelligent about this book, but obviously the gates of wisdom don't want to open up for me.<br /><br />* Also, a comment that I had to delete because of highly offensive vulgarity, mentioned something about Justine being a rape victim. I don't think that that is relevant in this book at all, taking into consideration that this book was not written by anyone who gives a rat's a** about rape victims. It was written as a "rape dream", as the author, an infamous pervert, did not want a real victim, he wanted a perversion that is both innocent and unwilling, and, at the same time, all too willing to be had by hordes of men. I think that it is very easy to see how there is a huge difference between the fate of a victim and the fate of a woman who is entirely fabricated to be pleasing to abusers. Therefore, the rape argument would be completely valid in every other situation, except for one where the purpose of the character is to be a daft cow who wants it. More so, I think it's offensive to real victims for Justine to be called one, as de Sade wanted her to be a wretch for the purpose of his own perversity. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6219539180284946240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3373896040" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3373896040" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48011083-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542289434p2/48011083.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3373896040?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/48011083-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3373896040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer852896107213675874">Continuing my curious journey into the work of fearless, imprisoned French authors, I come to this controversial volume, a highly unbalanced contest between Virtue and Vice, really Vice's mauling of Virtue. Yes, I felt much reluctance, even trepidatious shame, for what will my ‘friends’ think of my latest undertaking, delving into a long-proscribed text with a deviant reputation.<br/><br/>I believe this uncomfortable, terrifying work, written in the uncomfortable, terrifying era of the French revolution, </span>
  <span id="freeText852896107213675874" style="display:none">Continuing my curious journey into the work of fearless, imprisoned French authors, I come to this controversial volume, a highly unbalanced contest between Virtue and Vice, really Vice's mauling of Virtue. Yes, I felt much reluctance, even trepidatious shame, for what will my ‘friends’ think of my latest undertaking, delving into a long-proscribed text with a deviant reputation.<br /><br />I believe this uncomfortable, terrifying work, written in the uncomfortable, terrifying era of the French revolution, can be admired and condemned through many different lenses, which I’ll leave for others to consider. The question I’m left with, though, is why was this work suppressed? What individuals, what institutions, were advantaged in having this book stamped taboo? The answer appears to be the very same persons and institutions, named within, that have committed abuses beyond the public eye for centuries, the Church, the penal system, public authorities, parents, medical providers, schools, etc. I wonder, if this work were widely read over the years, and appropriately embedded in our collective conscious, would much have changed for the better? Don’t we minimize the threats from the evils that lurk in the hearts of man, and woman, in admitting and attempting to remedy, rather than repressing, the thoughts for those same evils?<br /><br />In March 2020, an acquaintance of mine, a retired schoolteacher, lamenting the stay-at-home orders rolling across America, said, “Oh, this is so, so sad.” “Why?” I asked, somewhat puzzled at her mournful tone. “Oh, for all the poor children and spouses that are about to be abused,” she replied. She knows something, I think. A shame we really have not come to terms with this situation in the past 230 years.</span>
  <a data-text-id="852896107213675874" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3415392335" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3415392335" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3415392335?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

          marked it as read-in-2020


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780965208284" href="/book/show/583970.Justine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=18th-century">18th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=erotica">erotica</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=proto-surrealism">proto-surrealism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3415392335" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer39391070338983125">Here was some necessary background reading so that I could finally dive into Angela Carter's book about pornography and De Sade, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/276751.The_Sadeian_Woman_And_the_Ideology_of_Pornography' title='The Sadeian Woman And the Ideology of Pornography by Angela Carter' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sadeian Woman</a>, also casting some light back on a great Rikki Ducornet essay, and endless other reference points in 19th and 20th century literature. Of course, this is boring and gratuitous like all pornographic writing, even at points horrifying though 18th century circumspection and euphemism make for some almost amusingly oblique flowery wording, but finally rea</span>
  <span id="freeText39391070338983125" style="display:none">Here was some necessary background reading so that I could finally dive into Angela Carter's book about pornography and De Sade, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/276751.The_Sadeian_Woman_And_the_Ideology_of_Pornography" title="The Sadeian Woman And the Ideology of Pornography by Angela Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sadeian Woman</a>, also casting some light back on a great Rikki Ducornet essay, and endless other reference points in 19th and 20th century literature. Of course, this is boring and gratuitous like all pornographic writing, even at points horrifying though 18th century circumspection and euphemism make for some almost amusingly oblique flowery wording, but finally reading it does underscore why de Sade has held lasting relevance. Here is a notable act of semi-sublimation -- the conversion of one's personal obsessions, in relatively unfiltered form, into something that can yet make a case for some sort of literary value. The story is simple: Justine, an archetypal virtuous orphan, must undergo an endless litany of misfortunes and abuses in a picaresque that carries her through all layers of an uncaring society. The specific stations of the passion of Justine are pure sadistic fantasy, culminating in a long stretch in a secret monastery devoted to sexual torture, it's impossible to imagine writing such a thing without genuine interest in exploring the mechanics of such <i>ad</i> (to all other readers) <i>nauseum</i>, and De Sade even goes so far as to couch these scenes in elaborate philosophical anti-discourse wherein Justine's arguments for traditional morality and religious piety are swamped by various libertines self-justifications of the inherent corruptness of society and the illusory qualities of morals as defense mechanism (some of which feel like they might have been right at home in Nietzsche a century later). But is this really what De Sade felt in the matter? For all the death and violence in here, and the abuses he inflicted in his personal life, it's worth noting that he was imprisoned during the French Revolution for moderatism -- for opposing the death penalty (Ducournet notes). And his case here is less that all desires are justified, then, perhaps, that the limp platitudes with which the poor are equipped to deal with their lot by religion and conventional moral learning are absolutely inadequate protection against the monstrous desires and abuses of society and its ruing classes. Justine armors herself in self righteousness, but will never strike back against her abusers or take anything for herself. Thus she is immolated again and again for failure to change her behavior or adapt to sordid circumstance. Like the novel's subtitle "The Misfortunes of Virtue", it's a parody of the a whole genre of lugubrious moral literature in which passive goodness prevails. In format, it's a detournement of Candide and "all is for the best in the best of all possible worlds". In construction it's at great pains to demonstrate the hypocritical unification of every strata of power in oppression, as Justine is attacked, in turn, by the clergy, petty businessmen, nobles, an esteemed surgeon, an entire religious order backed up by the full apparatus of the Church (one of whom is later granted immunity when he ascends to an even higher post), a vampiric lord who literalize the metaphor, and, at last, by the very judge who is to see Justine's case in the morning, as she is not only victimized by individuals but by the very apparatuses of society supposedly designed for her protection. As such, it's a scathing social critique. Why couch it in smut then? Perhaps to get people to read it (and keep it surreptitiously in print for two centuries!) or perhaps because in order to work up the motivation to write it, De Sade needed to amuse himself first of all. Probably both. Such is this weird brick of troubling, intelligent, acerbic material. Is it worth reading De Sade in 2020? Only you can answer that. Maybe it takes a fair amount of wading, maybe it's all too obvious anyway, but at core -- as current battles continue to reveal the papered-over tyranny of power, and as power continues to flaunt its own invulnerability to the checks and balances our society supposedly rests on -- this remains all too relevant. And yes, I recognize the irony of invoking De Sade, who abused his own power to feed his desires in an also all-too-familiar manner, in service of any modern social good. He would not likely be a good spokesperson for anything were he alive to today. As an artifact of history persisting to throw its shadow over the present, though, I acknowledge the interest here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="39391070338983125" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer68180384" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6297799615990077337">This is primarily a book of philosophy...which is not to say that it isn't also filled with fairly graphic (in an 18th century kind of way) scenes of every kind of sex act imanginable. The philosophy is that of the merits of vice vs. virtue. The protagonist (a virtuous woman) falls prey to a series of libertines who use and abuse her in whatever ways they deem pleasurable to themselves. Some of these tribulations are inflicted as the result of chance events while others result from Justine/There</span>
  <span id="freeText6297799615990077337" style="display:none">This is primarily a book of philosophy...which is not to say that it isn't also filled with fairly graphic (in an 18th century kind of way) scenes of every kind of sex act imanginable. The philosophy is that of the merits of vice vs. virtue. The protagonist (a virtuous woman) falls prey to a series of libertines who use and abuse her in whatever ways they deem pleasurable to themselves. Some of these tribulations are inflicted as the result of chance events while others result from Justine/Therese's exercise of her virtue, but each of her abusers spends a good deal of time explaining to her why the exercise of their vices is their right, is inevitable, and cannot be thwarted by appeals to virtue. The constant falling of Therese into peril reminded me of the storyline Voltaire employed in attacking the philosophy of "the best of all possible worlds" in <i>Candide</i>. It's hard to tell throughout the book on which side of the vice/virtue argument de Sade falls but most people - based on "conventional wisdom" - assume that it's on the side of vice. The ending to this story will surprise you. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6297799615990077337" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2427762614" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2427762614" />
    <a title="Amedine Amedine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30800272-amedine-amedine"><img alt="Amedine Amedine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594806797p2/30800272.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2427762614?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amedine Amedine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amedine Amedine" href="/user/show/30800272-amedine-amedine">Amedine Amedine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30800272-amedine-amedine?shelf=epic">epic</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2427762614" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7236605548910818184">let's leave all what was written about the author, introductions, criticism and rumors. because all these things mean nothing. If we want to get to know someone, we ought to read his work with neutrality and try to understand the message that he is trying to send us. <br/><br/>Because if the author was so evil and obscene as most people describe him. He wouldn't wrote what he wrote and he wouldn't share his thoughts with the world. And we shouldn't forget that he lived in an era where the church governed </span>
  <span id="freeText7236605548910818184" style="display:none">let's leave all what was written about the author, introductions, criticism and rumors. because all these things mean nothing. If we want to get to know someone, we ought to read his work with neutrality and try to understand the message that he is trying to send us. <br /><br />Because if the author was so evil and obscene as most people describe him. He wouldn't wrote what he wrote and he wouldn't share his thoughts with the world. And we shouldn't forget that he lived in an era where the church governed societies. To them Marquis de sade is a big threat to their filthy history. His thoughts are a destructive weapon against them. And if put in the hands of french people then. It will reveal for them the other side of the church. And that is what we see clearly in chapter 10.<br /><br />"The poor may get rich by stealing from the rich, because the rich get richer by stealing from the poor."<br /><br />"Thank God because the teachings of my religion will not give up on me, and because it made me suffer in this life"<br /><br />Those teachings according to Marquis provoke lust and restlessness in the lost souls, feed their hunger for control and power, and push their class in society to take over Justine's class: Those teachings make Justine beg and feel powerless, subconsciously and consciously, and then drive her to negative experiences because her thinking is negative. And maybe the author revealed an important part of his experience with religion in Justine's character, Despite the painful events, He carried her gently through the story, The reader can identify the sympathy of the author with Justine.<br /><br />Chapter 10 gives insights about what happens inside the church, these insights are not fiction for sure. It must be that the author witnessed those practices and rules. Each priest has his own women.. To be honest it was difficult to read the chapter, because it shows how women were used in a way that is not acceptable.<br /><br />And what the reader cannot miss is, Justine's insistence on her beliefs, and that insistence is simply a resistance against who takes away her freedom and abuse her rights and use her in any way possible.<br /><br />And this freedom that she is looking for in insisting on her religion is just efforts to break the chains, and because she is not aware of that, ignorant about that. She was used by her society to abandon her virtue, The virtue that seems to her as the foundation of her life and her absolute nature. <br />It is clear that Justine was ignorant and uneducated, her fear of giving up on her virtue is the same fear of giving up on her identity. Because she accepted her society's identity, therefore the society dictates with its religion and rulers the scenario of her life, and impulsively the situations that she goes through.<br />Those who created virtue knew it was a bait to their victims and preys. Unfortunately Justine was a victime of her virtue, because their cruelty and lack of compassion are coming from knowing the falseness of that virtue. Because they are the ones who did put its principles.<br /><br />If Justine never believed in virtue, she wouldn't beg each time she was throwing herself at their feet. She would have been more proud, more powerful and courageous.<br /><br /><br />That is why we witness her loss of control in chapter 10 when the illusion collapsed in front of her. Here it is, home of virtue which she glorified puts down its mask, and she wished for death, because she somewhat realized the illusion that was created inside her mind.<br /><br />In chapter 14 Marquis writes a thesis on the tongue of his character which tortures his wife. To say the least, It is a description of women history and their place throughout the civilizations and generations. And I find it so odd that an Arab person reads this novel and shame the author for telling the truth? Is there anybody who denies the way we treat women in our society? do we dare to refuse the reality that the woman lives for centuries inside our organized and extreme societies? from humiliating to using and degrading her dignity and her talent.<br />These character's thoughts that represent the thinking of the history of most men. These thoughts for him are rooted beliefs. Like "If women did not exist men would live" and he forgets of course how he came to existence from the womb of a woman. This philosophy is adapted by most men from childhood till middle age. A woman has lack of brain and religion. A woman is created to be of service to us.<br /><br />I am surprised how can a woman criticize this work? how couldn't she see beyond the inhuman scenes, to realize that the author is defending her?<br /><br />It may seem for some people that this novel despises women and distort her place, but on the contrary. I see that every woman who is looking for freedom must read Justine, every woman wants to take back her power should read it and understand its message.<br /><br />In chapter 17, The vice character says to Justine: "It depends on you, if you lose your purpose I won't of course. Was it a mistake when I told you your life depends on you?"<br />And the reader begins to understand the scene after that sentence, which shows the genius of the author and what goal serves this novel and why he wrote it particularly this way.<br /><br />If you lose your purpose I won't of course, you feel with this phrase that Vice is talking with Virtue personified by Justine<br />In other words, there is no Vice if there is no Virtue<br />Which means that Justine, The more she forces herself into practicing her blind virtue that leads her to those cruel experiences, the more she finds mountains of vice in front of her which never move or shake.<br /><br />Because life depends on you "Justine" and not on the virtue that you worship<br /><br />That is why Vice told her "Was it a mistake when I told you your life depends on you?"<br /><br />It is like as if the author wanted to tell us that Vice and Virtue are two faces of the same coin. They are governed by law of attraction or law of dualism<br /><br />And for Justine, everything that she resists attracts<br />That is why Vice gave Justine a knife to cut her hang rope<br />She didn't call Virtue to save her. She saved herself.<br /><br />We finally discover that "If Justine lost her purpose, Vice will not lose its purpose" becomes "If Justine didn't lose her purpose, Vice would have lost its purpose"<br />Justine then would be free, creating her own reality without vice or virtue, without good and evil<br />Which makes Vice right when it said that Justine's life depends on her and what she believes<br /><br />The novel ends in a tragic way, but the hidden philosophy of life is obvious to the mind, and when the author denies what his characters did to the heroine, he lets us taste his philosophy and believe that he is just a daring writer who described his society, where vice and virtue rule, and he roots for virtue and its reasons, and he assures that everything has a reason in this life, and that the absolute design as we are parts of it, we can't fully grasp it, and we can't understand human crimes just because we see it against life, while criminals see it as life itself.<br /><br />It is not possible to avoid the greatness of this philosopher, despite all the things that was said about him, his philosophy and his understanding of life make you meditate, and go outside your shell. Because it is not enough to just scream this is cruelty this is a crime, it is forbidden it is not right.<br />And if the author freed himself from expectations and wrote about what people feared to see as a part of their reality, then this vice and virtue that he is talking about, both change their skin, and perspectives according to traditions, cultures, religions and nations.<br />Governments declare war in the name of virtue<br />Religions practice punishments in the name of virtue<br />Criminals build their black markets in the name of vice<br />Parents abuse their children in the name of virtue<br />rebellions seek freedom in the name of vice<br /><br />And you ask yourself in confusion: What is the difference between vice and virtue?<br /><br />And you may answer with millions of answers, and you may not, but what is clear like the sun on its day, is forcing yourself to answer or to be silent, according to rooted beliefs, that were and still born from your choices and experiences...</span>
  <a data-text-id="7236605548910818184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3464123539" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3464123539" />
    <a title="Aung Sett Kyaw Min" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52918291-aung-sett-kyaw-min"><img alt="Aung Sett Kyaw Min" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515573087p2/52918291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3464123539?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aung Sett Kyaw Min" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aung Sett Kyaw Min" href="/user/show/52918291-aung-sett-kyaw-min">Aung Sett Kyaw Min</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3464123539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5887663519193384772">Sade's villains don't simply force themselves on our courageous heroine. <br/><br/>No, each display of brute force and each iteration of amoral violence that Providence metes out to Justine is followed by a series of sophistical arguments in which the perpetrators attempt to justify their behavior by appealing to the fact of natural depravity ("I was born this way, nature is at fault, etc). To many of us moderns who are living in the long shadow cast by the Enlightenment, this curious quality of Sade's li</span>
  <span id="freeText5887663519193384772" style="display:none">Sade's villains don't simply force themselves on our courageous heroine. <br /><br />No, each display of brute force and each iteration of amoral violence that Providence metes out to Justine is followed by a series of sophistical arguments in which the perpetrators attempt to justify their behavior by appealing to the fact of natural depravity ("I was born this way, nature is at fault, etc). To many of us moderns who are living in the long shadow cast by the Enlightenment, this curious quality of Sade's libertines cannot but strike as unsettling. It is as if Sade is proposing, at the height of Enlightenment, and anticipating Nietzsche, that what we call reason is simply violence by different means; will to power folding unto itself and emerging ever more pathological. <br /><br />Thematically speaking, Justine can be thought of us as an idiosyncratic response to the story of Job, Boethius' Consolation of Philosophy, glorifying what the latter rejects, and Voltaire's Candide, without the latter's anti theistic overtones. For me, this novella reinforces Pierre Klossowski's thesis that Sade is a closet gnostic. Justine's fate shows that those who keep to the path of virtue will incur the wrath of the evil Supreme Being who revels in libidinal pandemonium. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5887663519193384772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2075615376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17629823967363181424">I don't even know how to rate or review this. Yes, I was well aware of de Sade's reputation before I started reading this and I knew what the subject matter would be like. If you don't know well, look it up (rape, torture, scat, vivisection, and worse). Despite the disturbing subject matter (and Justine isn't even considered one of his most depraved works - I believe that title belongs to 120 Days of Sodom), this book has stood the test of time and is considered by some, a classic. <br/><br/>I don't reall</span>
  <span id="freeText17629823967363181424" style="display:none">I don't even know how to rate or review this. Yes, I was well aware of de Sade's reputation before I started reading this and I knew what the subject matter would be like. If you don't know well, look it up (rape, torture, scat, vivisection, and worse). Despite the disturbing subject matter (and Justine isn't even considered one of his most depraved works - I believe that title belongs to 120 Days of Sodom), this book has stood the test of time and is considered by some, a classic. <br /><br />I don't really have the time to go full philosophical in this review, so I will review it like I do most books. The story was repetitive and frustrating. The actions of the main character were often stupid. The ‘sexual’ content, in my eyes, was so outlandish and unlikely that I didn’t even see it as sexual, rather just there for pure shock value. <br /><br />I liked the writing (or, I guess, translation), and I think I would like to sit down and do some research on de Sade and his works to learn more about his intentions and impact.<br /><br />Anyway, this book is not for the faint of heart.<br /></span>

    <a title="Luís" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s"><img alt="Luís" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598708286p2/117399210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Capa Mole " href="/book/show/6699689-justine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=2-stars-only">2-stars-only</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3415627842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8236336690045694468">This book is appalling. One expects a novel written on the subject of libertinism. It is all the worse: it is only women sequestered, subjected, under duress and threats, to the worst perversities of men. One is far beyond what one can imagine since imposed sex added to violence, blood, scatology, death.<br/>Those who admire Sade in the name of sexual liberation are all wrong: there is no liberation in Sade, but only coercion. Only perverse, violent men with dangerous, selfish tendencies, who dress t</span>
  <span id="freeText8236336690045694468" style="display:none">This book is appalling. One expects a novel written on the subject of libertinism. It is all the worse: it is only women sequestered, subjected, under duress and threats, to the worst perversities of men. One is far beyond what one can imagine since imposed sex added to violence, blood, scatology, death.<br />Those who admire Sade in the name of sexual liberation are all wrong: there is no liberation in Sade, but only coercion. Only perverse, violent men with dangerous, selfish tendencies, who dress their evil and criminal actions by theories which are only sophisms.<br />On the literary level, this book is, of course, an exciting subject. And the language of the eighteenth century is so beautiful! But what a painful moment, that the discovery of a book in which he is not free and benevolent character!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8236336690045694468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
