<div id="review_265010556" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265010556" />
    <a title="Dara Salley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4699853-dara-salley"><img alt="Dara Salley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400200476p2/4699853.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/265010556?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dara Salley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dara Salley" href="/user/show/4699853-dara-salley">Dara Salley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265010556" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6479778154622480691"> This is the first book I’ve read by the Marquis de Sade, but of course I’m aware of his reputation. His books have been perennially banned. Even today, “Justine” should be available on the public domain site ProjectGutenberg.com, but it’s censored from that site. I can understand why. “Justine” is a very unpleasant book. It was shocking to readers in the 1700’s and even in the 21st century world of prime-time zombie dismemberment and Nicky Minaj, it’s still pretty shocking. This book is the wor</span>
  <span id="freeText6479778154622480691" style="display:none"> This is the first book I’ve read by the Marquis de Sade, but of course I’m aware of his reputation. His books have been perennially banned. Even today, “Justine” should be available on the public domain site ProjectGutenberg.com, but it’s censored from that site. I can understand why. “Justine” is a very unpleasant book. It was shocking to readers in the 1700’s and even in the 21st century world of prime-time zombie dismemberment and Nicky Minaj, it’s still pretty shocking. This book is the work of an undoubtedly disturbed mind. It is such a passionate treatise on cruelty and immorality; it could only have come from someone who truly believed in what he was writing.<br /><br />The format of the novel is repetitive. Justine meets a man who seems to offer protection and sympathy, but they are quickly revealed to have sinister motives. She ends up in various lurid situations, many of which could not be shown today, even on HBO. There is always a pause, however, in the sadistic action, when the evil predator takes Justine into his confidence and explains the reasoning that allows him to engage in despicable behavior without qualms. This is where the novel comes alive. De Sade offers such intriguing rationalizations for evil; I couldn’t help but be amused. He lived in the 1700’s, a time that was all about morals, decorum and gentility. De Sade gleefully tears down the flimsy fabric of polite society and reveals the wicked, animalistic nature that lurks underneath. It’s disturbing, and he was considered a criminal in his time, but his point of view is undeniable unique. Maybe it’s important and genius too, despite being distasteful. I can’t say I would ever recommend that anyone read this book; I myself found it difficult to finish. However, it’s certainly a rare piece of work.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6479778154622480691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2696734980?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bon Tom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bon Tom" href="/user/show/40807670-bon-tom">Bon Tom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007300440" href="/book/show/6337204-justine">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2696734980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8406455849664307891">This book surprised me on all accounts.<br/><br/>I expected style too luxurious for its own good, like it's often the case with the classics, expected crude, though tamed, descriptions of sexuality. Some form of try hardiness in that regard.<br/><br/>I received none of that. This book is surprisingly well written, complex, ambitious in its scope and, at the same time, joy to read. There's abundant philosophical and religious side to it that makes you think, in pauses between daring descriptions of sex scenes. <br/><br/>Which</span>
  <span id="freeText8406455849664307891" style="display:none">This book surprised me on all accounts.<br /><br />I expected style too luxurious for its own good, like it's often the case with the classics, expected crude, though tamed, descriptions of sexuality. Some form of try hardiness in that regard.<br /><br />I received none of that. This book is surprisingly well written, complex, ambitious in its scope and, at the same time, joy to read. There's abundant philosophical and religious side to it that makes you think, in pauses between daring descriptions of sex scenes. <br /><br />Which are the most classic thing about this book. They were perpetuated hundreds of times after, in some form or the other. Books, comics, porn, they all use these ideas. I wonder if all the copycats have De Sade to thank to, or were they depicted somewhere even earlier?<br /><br />And historical context makes it even more impressive. It was light years ahead of its time. <br /><br />This book is still underrated, which is telling, in my opinion. Seems we are still decades, maybe even centuries from humanity being completely comfortable with the idea of intellectual freedom.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8406455849664307891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2407930710" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2407930710" />
    <a title="Connor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26180666-connor"><img alt="Connor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517643579p2/26180666.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_568780326" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/568780326" />
    <a title="Vanessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa"><img alt="Vanessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596200358p2/4636758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/568780326?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa" href="/user/show/4636758-vanessa">Vanessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199572847" href="/book/show/16182384-justine-or-the-misfortunes-of-virtue">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer568780326" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8967346764549434666"><i>Justine, or the Misfortunes of Virtue</i> was a challenging novel for me to read. I knew what I was getting myself into at the time I checked it out of the library, but it is maybe telling that it was one of the few library rentals that I had to consistently renew for months before I eventually decided to open its pages.<br/><br/>This novel is part violent pornography, and part philosophical debate, which makes for an interesting if initially bizarre mix. It tells the story of the young heroine Justine, relat</span>
  <span id="freeText8967346764549434666" style="display:none"><i>Justine, or the Misfortunes of Virtue</i> was a challenging novel for me to read. I knew what I was getting myself into at the time I checked it out of the library, but it is maybe telling that it was one of the few library rentals that I had to consistently renew for months before I eventually decided to open its pages.<br /><br />This novel is part violent pornography, and part philosophical debate, which makes for an interesting if initially bizarre mix. It tells the story of the young heroine Justine, relating the tragic circumstances of her life to travellers as she is on her way to be executed for murder, theft, and arson. Orphaned at twelve, she separates from her fifteen year old sister Juliette (who has libertine tendencies), and from this young age to the ultimate age of around twenty-seven, she is continuously subjected to various degrading, torturous, and humiliating experiences at the hands of various high-born (and occasionally low-born) men (and occasionally women)... but for the most part high-born men.<br /><br />The violence in this book increased as the novel went on, and although I felt I was coping quite well with it at the start, there were certain sections that made me feel progressively more ill and more uncomfortable. Some scenes were very difficult to read, and I will not recount any specific events here. In general though, be prepared to find everything from paedophilia to sadomasochism here, and everything in between. If you think you can handle that, by all means give this book a whirl. If you know you can't, I wouldn't pick this up.<br /><br />Marquis de Sade was clearly inspired by various other libertine novelists at the time, and his writing is impressive throughout (if at times a little convoluted). However, the weakness here lies in his characterisation, of Justine in particular. She is portrayed initially as the typical blonde bimbo character, beautiful and naive and obsessed with virtue and her own virginity. The novel becomes increasingly more and more ridiculous as she gets herself time and time again into horrible situations, due to her trusting of the various men she meets along her travels. I found it completely laughable that a young girl who had been subjected to the horrors she had previously would continuously accept help from random strange men, and not even be a little suspicious. Ultimately Justine's incredible naivety would lead her into unbelievable (and laughable) situations. <br /><br />I think the best way to deal with this book when reading it is to treat it as a farcical tale that could never be realised. Thinking of the characters (Justine, and her supremely evil wrongdoers) as exactly that, characters, is probably the best way of coping with the shudder-inducing violent sexual and physical acts portrayed here. <br /><br />On a side note, let it be said that the introduction to this particular edition by the translator John Phillips was extremely insightful. I read the history of the Marquis de Sade's life before I read the main text, and then went back to read the spoiler-full commentary on the text and its writing style after I had finished. The explanatory notes section he included at the back was also very useful for me, and I could flip back and forth to read the annotations with ease. These helped increase my understanding of certain references scattered throughout (and helped me to recognise when de Sade had plagiarised, a very common thing!). <br /><br />Although I didn't really enjoy this book much at all, I couldn't give it 1 star. The Marquis de Sade's writing is to be commended, and his intelligence really comes through here, even if his imagination is frightful at times. It was also interesting to think of some sections of Justine's narrative as mirroring his own sense of injustice while imprisoned during the writing of the novel. However, I don't think I'll be picking up <i>The 120 Days of Sodom</i> any time soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8967346764549434666" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('n1aiDamffqeWPLaLb0J29xsttHsd0r9OIVig06cyS8E9Rhrj+abZLcU1zpHId4Dhst/ljFPpUS4fFocUty1kPQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FC161FfL+kB52F4vEJfVZPWN2PT+TO2qG65eXgk/iGC2PcI6B/JdyirRJjW3oiNyXH+JA7B3A8ol4HmZGSCnnA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

It is a long narrative prose poem which celebrates the principle of Evil in a<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12237189084283084850\" style=\"display:none\">The macabre but beautiful work, Les Chants de Maldoror, has achieved a considerable reputation as one of the earliest and most extraordinary examples of Surrealist writing. It is a long narrative prose poem which celebrates the principle of Evil in an elaborate style and with a passion akin to religious fanaticism. The French poet-critic Georges Hugnet has written of Lautréamont: &quot;He terrifies, stupefies, strikes dumb. He could look squarely at that which others had merely given a passing glance.&quot;\n\n\nLittle is known of the author of Maldoror, Isidore Ducasse, self-styled Comte de Lautréamont, except that he was born in Montevideo, Uruguay in 1846 and died in Paris at the age of twenty-four. When first published in 1868-9, Maldoror went almost unnoticed. But in the nineties the book was rediscovered and hailed as a work of genius by such eminent writers as Huysmans, Léon Bloy, Maeterlinck, and Rémy de Gourmont. Later still, Lautréamont was to be canonized as one of their principal &quot;ancestors&quot; by the Paris Surrealists.\n\n\nThis edition, translated by Guy Wernham, includes also a long introduction to a never-written, or now lost, volume of poetry. Thus, except for a few letters, it gives all the surviving literary work of Lautréamont.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"12237189084283084850\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return Deleuze\'s essay, certainly the most profound study yet produced on the relations between sadism and masochism, seeks to develop and explain Masoch\'s &quot;peculiar way of \'desexualizing\' love while at the same time sexualizing the entire history of humanity.&quot; He shows that masochism is something far more subtle and complex than the enjoyment of pain, that masochism has nothing to do with sadism; their worlds do not communicate, just as the genius of those who created them - Masoch and Sade - lie stylistically, philosophically, and politically poles a part. Venus in Furs, the most famous of all of Masoch\'s novels was written in 1870 and belongs to an unfinished cycle of works that Masoch entitled The Heritage of Cain. The cycle was to treat a series of themes including love, war, and death. The present work is about love. Although the entire constellation of symbols that has come to characterize the masochistic syndrome can be found here - fetishes, whips, disguises, fur-clad women, contracts, humiliations, punishment, They present a world of sensation in which only the vaulting demands of disruptive excess and the anguish of heightened awareness can combat the stultifying world of reason and social order. Each of the narratives contains a sense of intoxication and insanity so carefully delineated by the author that it seems to infect the reader.Philosopher, novelist and critic, Georges Bataille is a major figure in twentieth-century literature whose startling and original ideas increasingly exert a vital influence on the shaping of thought, language and experience. Best known outside France for the vertiginous sexual delirium of his short novel, Story of the Eye, the vast scope of Bataille\'s interests and intellect made him a major force in many spheres.Bataille\'s essays range over such diverse topics as economics, psychoanalysis, Marxism, yoga and anthropology. His critical essays, Literature and Evil and his complex meditations on the dark coupling of sex and death, Eroticism, are both available from Marion Boyars.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2885224.Marquis_de_Sade">About Marquis de Sade</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<span id="freeTextContainer12016977951546146083">Donatien Alphonse François, Marquis de Sade (1740-1814) was a French aristocrat, revolutionary politician, philosopher, and writer famous for his libertine sexuality and lifestyle.<br/><br/>His works include novels, short stories, plays, dialogues, and political tracts; in his lifetime some were published under his own name, while others appeared anonymously and Sade denied being their author. He is best kn</span>
  <span id="freeText12016977951546146083" style="display:none">Donatien Alphonse François, Marquis de Sade (1740-1814) was a French aristocrat, revolutionary politician, philosopher, and writer famous for his libertine sexuality and lifestyle.<br /><br />His works include novels, short stories, plays, dialogues, and political tracts; in his lifetime some were published under his own name, while others appeared anonymously and Sade denied being their author. He is best known for his erotic works, which combined philosophical discourse with pornography, depicting sexual fantasies with an emphasis on violence, criminality, and blasphemy against the Catholic Church. He was a proponent of extreme freedom, unrestrained by morality, religion or law.<br />Sade was incarcerated in various prisons and in an insane asylum for about 32 years of his life; eleven years in Paris (10 of which were spent in the Bastille) a month in Conciergerie, two years in a fortress, a year in Madelonnettes, three years in Bicêtre, a year in Sainte-Pélagie, and 13 years in the Charenton asylum. During the French Revolution he was an elected delegate to the National Convention. Many of his works were written in prison.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12016977951546146083" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/13268607-justine-ou-les-malheurs-de-la-vertu">Quotes from Justine, or The M...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Were he supreme, were he mighty, were he just, were he good, this God you tell me about, would it be through enigmas and buffooneries he would wish to teach me to serve and know him?&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/263639-were-he-supreme-were-he-mighty-were-he-just-were">22 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I am a libertine, but I am not a criminal nor a murderer, and since I am compelled to set my apology alongside my vindication, I shall therefore say that it might well be possible that those who condemn me as unjustly as I have been might themselves be unable to offset the infamies by good works as clearly established as those I can contrast to my errors. And yet you who today tyrannize me so cruelly, you do not believe it either: your vengeance has beguiled your mind, you have proceeded blindly to tyrannize, but your heart knows mine, it judges it more fairly, and it knows full well it is innocent.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/704960-i-am-a-libertine-but-i-am-not-a-criminal">15 likes</a>
